Seduction through the Ozm Zither
(Enter Pan Bizheng.)

Pan sings:

How beautiful the moon is on this clear and dewy night!
Sleepless and listless, I languish in nature’ autumn song.
In my mind are the ancient poet’s words on the west wind,
And all the fallen leaves: fragments of my idle dreams!

(Recitative:)
Pan:

My name is Pan Bizheng, Having failed the provincial
examination, I was too ashamed to return home. So

here I am, visiting my aunt at her nunnery, where I
came to meet Ms. Chen Miaochang, the young lady
preparing to become a nun. You know when we met,
I could distinctly sense some tender feelings she felt
for me. See the quiet courtyard flooded with the
intoxicating moonlight, I think [ll go take a stroll
around White Cloud Chamber, where Ms. Chen stays.
Pl do just that.
Pan sings:

Stro/ling along, I see the fallen petals on the path.

(Exit Pan. Enter Chen Miaochang.)
Chen sings: See how reflections of the blossoms dance on the wall,
A\s bamboo trees and withered lotus sway in the autumn
breexe.
Oh, yes, to the bright moon will I play my xither,
Ass the gold incense burner revels in fragrance.
(Recitative:)
Chen:

My name is Chen Miaochang. Lately ve been so
occupied with all the mundane affairs in the nunnery
that I’ve quite neglected practicing my zither. It’s
such a lovely, refreshing moon tonight. Let me play
something dedicated to this sweet evening.

Chen sings: Indeed, I feel as if I were in the celestial palace.
(Reenter Pan.)

Pan sings:

Moving quietly, I pass through the empty galleries,

Wait! Is it the wind I’m hearing or...how intriguing!
A xither, yes, the sound intoxicating.
Who's playing so adroitly - who can it be?
Is it played to the moon, or to melancholy?

Chen sings: Alas, my sadness will not be dispelled with the music.

(Recitative by Pan: The door is ajar. Ah, it’s the maiden nun-to-be
Chen Miaochang, playing the zither. Let me enter.)
All I can do is to sigh to the autumn wind.
(Recitative by Pan: Brava, brava!)
How tis it that you, young master Pan, are here in my
quarters?
You have given me quite a start!
(Recitative:)
Pan:

Chen:

Oh, please forgive me for startling you, Miss. I was
just strolling about.
Young master Pan:

Chen sings: So this cool autumn night's music has brought you here.
(Recitative:)
Pan:

Indeed. I was drawn by the exquisite songs and your
clear, beautiful voice accompanied by the zither.

Chen:

Oh, you flattered me. The lovely moon and the cool
evening air stirred me. But I was singing and playing
quite crudely.

Pan:

That is not true, Miss.

Chen:

I have long heard that you, Master Pan, are an
exemplary singer, and a master of the zither. Would
you be so kind as to sing and play for me a few
passages?

Pan:

But I wouldn’t dare to perform to you, with you
being such a marvelous singer and player.

Chen:

You’re much too modest, I’m sure. I humbly request
that you sing and play a little.

Pan:

Sit down, please.

Thank you...My, what a fine zither!

Pan sings:

See how the male pheasant sails through the autumn
skies!
Without a mate, without a love, in loneliness he flies!

How, pray, can I ever find a darling to join me on my
Journey?
No answer is forthcoming; so I remain in misery, oh, in
misery!

(Recitative:)
Chen:

Bravo!

Pan:

I hope you'll forgive me for my poor performance.

Chen:

It was “A Lonely Male Pheasant,” wasn’t it? I don’t see
why you, being so young, should feel sad about not
finding a wife.

Pan:

Indeed I don’t have a wife.

Chen:

Oh well, that is really no affair of mine!

Pan:

Would you, my dear miss, sing and play another song?

Chen:

But Sir:
Celestial sound was created by thee a joy for a worldly creature like me.
No need now for my crude playing to ruin
echoes of thy music so heavenly!

Pan:

Now YOU are too modest. I insist.

Chen:

But I’m afraid that my music might offend you.

Pan:

Oh, no, not at all!

Chen:

Please sit down.

Pan:

Thanks.

Chen:

Since you insist, Pll make a fool of myself and play.

The Jade Pin

Chen sings: See the floating clouds so light in the sky, mixing
gossamer and mist;
On earth the rich scents of the incense and the cassia
smell so sweet!

When will this long, lonesome night draw to an end?
Not to me wist!

(Recitative by Pan: Marvelously played! Chen: Sit down, please! )

Chen sings: The fair moon is my only companion, and to her I greet,
yes, I greet!
(Recitative:)
Pan:

Brava!

Chen:

I performed poorly.

Pan:

Not at all, Miss. That was “Frolicking in the Moon,”

frequently performed by Buddhist nuns, wasn’t it? It
was good. How true that all the nights are too long
and lonesome, as the song suggests?
Chen:

But we are quite accustomed to it, Master Pan.

Pan:

That is not necessarily true, Miss.

Chen:

Oh, Master Pan, hear me:

Chen sings: The flowing waters of rivers, whether long or short,
bear no human feelings.
Natural elements in the universe, be they clouds or water,
exist without human worries.
Whether it’s spring that has arrived,
Or its the blossoms that have faded,
They stir up no emotions in my heart;
For my heart is as peaceful as still water.
At night, I listen but to bells in the nunnery;
During the day, I smell but incense in the altar.
My Life is now devoid of any worldly thoughts.
(Recitative by Pan: What a pure and celestial young lady, so eager
to be a nun!)
Chen:

So eager to become a nun, | am,
A pious Buddhist spirit praised by everyone,
And criticized by no one!

Pan sings:

All sounds, whether the waterclock or hourglass,

Ass you claim, are to you sounds not of human beings,
But the sounds I heard, both zither and sweet voice,

Betrayed the feelings deep in the heart of a maiden.
You know even a satin-quilt can still be cold when it’s
not shared by two;
As a blossoming flower can be lonely without the company
of the moon.
Oh, my dear pious Nun-To-Be, can you really bear a life
of solitude,
Or can you bear the coldness in bed, shared not with any
gentleman?
(Recitative:)
Chen:

How boldly and vulgarly you’ve spoken, Master Pan!
Truly, you have offended me! I shall go and report
this to your aunt, the Abbess.

Pan sings:

Oh, my dear miss, please pardon me.
Indeed, I have spoken quite rudely.
I spoke inappropriately all because of love,
Which led to my shameful disgrace!
But being the gentle soul you are, Miss,
Youll forgive me, an impetuous man, I hope.

(Recitative:)
Pan:

In order not to offend you more, I bid you good night.

Chen:

You must forgive me too, if I’ve offended you.

Pan:

Oh, not at all, Miss.

(To himself): What an inexorably hard person she is!
Chen (To herself): Oh, I can NOT say he was entirely wrong in his
observation.

Pan (To himself): So we part so unpleasantly. How [ll be thinking
about her from tonight on!
Chen:

Please watch your steps among the flower bushes.

Pan:

Lend me yout lantern, then, Miss.

Chen:

Pah!

The Jade Pin

Pan (To himself): Wait! I think she’s fond of me, but she is playing
a game. Let me hide in the bushes and listen to what
she has to say.
(Exit Pan.)

Chen:

It’s all quiet now. I guess he’s gone. Yes, gone! Oh,
Master Pan, my sweet man...!

Chen sings: My, he’s so pleasing to the eye, and what romantic
disposition!
Now serious, now teasing,

He smiles so sweetly!
Certainly, I know his thoughts,
But, I had to feign annoyance,
And I had to reject him with hard words.
Oh, how Id like to accept his love, but how could I?

What agony when I had to feign indifference every time I
saw him,

And to harbor this romantic yearning through each
subsequent interim!
Oh, what a waste, this charming moon and all the lovely

blossoms!
All I feel now is sadness, watching him saunter off so
despondently!
Yes, I feel none but this pervasive lonesomeness and
heartache!
(Recitative:)
Chen:

It’s getting late. I had better turn in. Oh, all this love
is felt but in vain.

Back alone in my moonlit room,
How I shall shed endless tears of gloom.

(Recitative:)
Pan:

A-hah!

Chen:

Oh, dear! Oh, dear god!

(Exit Chen.)
Pan:

Ha, ha! I heard most of what she was saying.

Pan sings:

(Recitative:)
Pan:

Every word she spoke belied her deep feelings.
Every gesture, each expression
Showed the life of a nun unappealing.
Oh, your zither play, so tender and fine,
Makes me wish to die for you, dear miss!
Like the purest of jade, sweetest of scents,
Youve touched the very core of my being!
Now in her room she’s alone, all by herself,
On this cold, mossy path, I too am a lonely soul.
I know we are destined to be as one in this life.
Oh, how Iyearn for us to be husband and wife!
I had better return. Oh, dear, it’s getting really cold!

Visiting the Sick
(Enter Abbess.)
Abbess sings: Suddenly 7 and laid up in his room,
My nephew must be in a mood of gloom.
(Enter Chen Miaochang,)
(Recitative:)
Chen:

I’m paying respect to you, Abbess.

Abbess:

My dear disciple, ’m going to the study to look in on
my sick nephew.

Chen:

Abbess, I’d like to come with you.

Abbess:

That would be fine. Having failed his examination,

my nephew is ill from his disgrace.
Chen:

And I’m quite certain he would rather hide his
gloomy face!

(Enter Peaceful.)
(Recitative:)
Peaceful:

§There’re medicines for all ailments, I’m sure For love-sickness, though, there is not one cure!

I am Peaceful, servant-boy to Master Pan Bizheng.
What a laugh all this is: my master, after failing the
examination, came to this nunnery of his aunt’s to get
over his disgrace. What do you know, here he came
to meet Ms. Chen, his nemesis of love. Oh, how

outrageously they flirt with one another all day long giving each other the eye and all! And lo and behold,
before you know it, my master has come down with
this love-sickness, which no medicine can cure! What’s to

be done, indeed! Let me help him out for some fresh
air - he may then feel a little better, I sure hope.
Peaceful:

Say, Master. My Master!

Peaceful:

Oh, Abbess, but in no one can my poor master
confide his agony!

Pan sings:

Never before an illness like this befell me,
An illness that is causing so much misery!
It makes me feel like the fallen leaves in a northwester,

It has made me move like blossoms after a rain shower!
(Recitative by Abbess: But what’s on your mind, Child?
Oh, unspeakable pain, Abbess.)

Peaceful:

I tried hard to shake it, But it returned to torture!

And twilight seems like the saddest moment,
When I fret about my predicament!
(Recitative by Peaceful: Boy, can he talk about his illness!
Abbess: Tell me what’s on your mind, Child.)
There’ so much in my heart I cannot tell,
Only tears would flow: Oh, how I'm in hell!

of her affairs. Abbess: Did it start with a cold, my child?)
Though it may have some of the symptoms of a cold,

oh, what sadness!

Pan: No, it is not.

The Jade Pin

Love Through a Poem
(Enter Chen Miaochang,)
Chen sings: As, the west wind is here;

Gold chrysanthemum everywhere.
Human langourousness not felt by the wild birds,
They have come in pairs to the pond side.
(Recitative:) Ever since meeting Master Pan, P’ve been pervaded
with this nameless, faint feeling of daze.

The coming of the fall is not helping matter; for it
only makes me feel more lost.
It wasn’t my own wish to be a Buddhist nun.
I was compelled to come to this decision, only
because I had lost my family and home.
What should I do about this, and what is to become

of me?
Why don’t I write all this down in a poem? I’ll do just
that.
Hmm...Here it goes:

“The lone lamp twinkling in this nunnery in the pine
woods,

The bells and drums now quiet as day is falling,
Time to bed it is, and I am my bedding unrolling,

Yet sleep will not come, I know, in my current uneasy
moods.
How my mind is pervaded by some thoughts
disturbing:
Thoughts that have to do with love and romance.
Tried I have to rid of these thoughts with penance,
But they only get stronger, and can amount only to
nothing!”

Chen sings: To whom can I tell
So long each day seems to me?
This, I know, is an affliction of love.
Sleepless, I watch the moon rise and set;

Iam, at the White Cloud Chamber. What intoxicating
fragrance!
What a wonderful spot this is:
The green pines shine radiantly amongst the autumn
blossoms!

of mercy...Oh, no! She looks more

like Shennu,

the lovely goddess of love. How can anyone be
so bewitching even while asleep! I think I’m going
to tease her a bit...Wait, what if she gets angry? I
shouldn’t take the chance...Now, what’s written on

that piece of paper under her elbow, I wonder. Looks
like a poem she’s composed.Hm...I’ll take it and have
a look...Here I’ve got it. Let me read it,

“The lone lamp tainkling in this nunnery in the pine woods,
The bells and drums now quiet as day ts falling,”
So she writes like a nun. Let me read on.

A Forced Divorcement
(Enter Zhu Maichen.)
(Recitative:)
Zhu:

Sings:

Oh my, what cold weather!
An impoverished scholar Imay be,
I savor the joy studying classics gives me.
Yes, I've composed essays describing my indigence,
None of them, alas, improves my needy existence!

(Recitative:) My name is Zhu Maichen. I left home early this
morning for the Broken Axe Mountain to chop some
firewood to make a living. Here I am at the foot of
the mountain. But look, dark clouds are gathering

and chilly wind is lifting. ’m afraid it’s going to snow
any minute. Hardly a time for wood chopping, Pll
say. ’'d better be going home. I’m home. Look, the
broken door is blown wide open by the icy wind. Let
me enter. But wait! My wife Cui Shi has lately been
badgering me fiercely on account of our wretched
living conditions. Seeing that I didn’t chop any
firewood in exchange for rice, she is bound to be
furious. Oh, you luckless Zhu Maichen! Really you
don’t even deserve a wife, for you can’t even provide
her with food? I really can’t blame her for badgering
me, can I? She’s been suffering this poverty with me
for twenty years now. Who can blame her for feeling
so bitter against me? I say, Zhu Maichen. You'd better
be more understanding about her complaints. And
be very tolerant of her wrath! Say, ’m home, Cut

Shi, my wife! Now, where can she be? Must be at our
neighbor Mama Wang’s. Let me tend to my books. I’ve
wasted enough time not studying.
(Enter Cui Shi)

Sings:

Studying every day, hell be somebody someday - so dream
on, my man!

烂柯 山
Ballad of the Broken Axe Mountain

Twenty long years I've suffered this poverty since our
marriage began.
Mama Wang told me to drop him for Carpenter Chang,
the millionaire;

So Ive the paper to get me out of this hell I can no
longer bear!
(Recitative:)
Zhu:

Here you are! Wife dear!

Cui:

Zhu Maichen!

Zhu:

Yes, dear.

Cui:

So you dict get any firewood, did you?

Zhu:

I was just about to tell you, wife. When I got to the
foot of the mountain, clouds gathered and icy wind
lifted. It sure looked like it was going to snow any
minute. I knew it was impossible to get any firewood.

Cui:

I see. So it was impossible to get any firewood, hey?

Zhu:

I’m afraid so.

Cui:

Which means another day without any rice to eat!
Oh, my god!

Zhu:

Oh, not to worry, my dear wife. You see: there are
still a few grains of rice in the rice jar. Now why don’t
you cook them with plenty of water and make some
thin porridge. That should tide us over for today. I'll
do something tomorrow.

Cui:

What rubbish!

Zhu:

Oh, dear, how sinful of you to throw rice away like
that! Haven’t you read it in the classics?
“Every grain of rice in a bowl
Comes from field labor strenuous and toilsome.
Oh, what high price, do you not know,

A farmer has to pay for a life wholesome!”

I’m sure came up with this idea of a divorce. And

bill of divorcement has to say: “I am Zhu Maichen
of the Kuaiji Prefecture; by trade I am a woodcutter.
I can NOT provide for my poor wife; so I hereby
grant a divorce to her.” Did you hear that? So, Zhu
Maichen, from this day on you and I will each go our
own way, and never, ever to see each other again!

You ARE callous about this!

Cui:

I suppose I am.

Zhu:

Oh heavens, am I really a destitute scholar!

Cui:

A gutless bum is more like it! Now, Master Zhu!

Put down your fingerprints!

Oh my! You’ve been my woman almost half of my life;

wife!
How heartless

Husband and wife for twenty long years,

Zhu:

Wait! My wife, my dear, do you mean to say that this

divorcement!

Cui:

Let go! I say let go! Zhu Maichen, Zhu Maichen!
Now listen to me, Zhu Maichen. Don’t you blame
me for all this! All you ever cared about was to study
your classics, YOUR CLASSICS! Did you care that I,
your wife, was cold and hungry for so long? No, you
didn’t! As the old saying goes, “Why marry a man,
why marry a man? For provide clothes he can, for
provide food he can!” But not you! When did you
ever provide for me? Why, I wonder, did you ever
marry me? I’m not so heartless as you said. I’m just
a human who tries to live a little and not to die of
poverty! I'l tell you what. I’m leaving you this piece
of silver given to me by someone. You can have it!
Leave me alone from this point on, and don’t ever
come looking for me! Hear this and remember:
As a wife for twenty years, I was good to you;
It is for survival that I bid you adieu!

(Exit Cui Shi.)
Zhu:

My wife, my wife! So she’s really gone! Oh me, oh my!

(Exit Zhu Maichen.)

decision to divorce him back then! You would be on
cloud nine now with the news of his becoming such

a high official! For you would surely be a grand lady
of title...But alas, as it is, I couldn’t think of going to
him -

Cui sings:
(Recitative:)
Cui:

For I've become so shabby and mangy in sight;

What’s

more...how does that saying go...“Husband and wife

if but for a night/One hundred days will their hearts
unite.”

Sings:

Yes, he may still recall our moments of love and play,
And overlook the awkwardness between us today.

(Enter Block Chief.)

(Recitative:) Block Chief is but a poor fellow,
Busy without a moment free.
And when high officials drop by,
He’s even busier than a bee!

Tam the block chief in this small town at the foot of
the Broken Axe Mountain. I’ve been notified that
Zhu Maichen, once a woodcutter from our town, was

now appointed - believe it or not - the new Governor
of this prefecture! And, on his way to the Prefectural
capital of Kuaiji, Master Zhu is passing through this
town today. I’d better tell all the reputed citizens,
the wine sellers, the butchers, the cooks and the

Get ready to welcome him!

(Enter Chen Jichang.)
(Recitative:)
Jichang:

Says who lionesses stay only in jungle?
As mine roars in my own house,
I tremble miserably in my heart!

Yours truly goes by the name of Chen Jichang.
Having studied many classics and poetry, I enjoy
some fine reputation of being a scholar. A married
man, I do appreciate my wife Liu Shi who is not
only blessed with a lovely countenance, but also with
considerable literary talent. The only problem is,
hm...how should I put it? She is so unduly sensitive
and suspicious, and what jealousy! There seems
nothing I can do except enduring it.
(Liu Shi makes an utterance from backstage.)
Oh dear, she’s coming. Let me greet her respectfully.
(Liu Shi enters.)
So languorous in my springtime dream,
I'm awakened by the singing orioles,
A\s outside of my green-gauzed window
the sun moves shadows of the blossoms.

(Butler and Su Dongpo enter.)
Susings:

— Floating on this worldly sea of bureaucracy,
I have been here and there, drifting all these years,
Though with my spirit of a bon vivant unbent.
Expressing myself through poetry and wine,
Ive composed at all corners of this land.

(Enter Fift.)

Fifisings:

_A// my rich patrons interest me not,
For poets and literati are my favorites.

Making the most of this fine, spring day,
We are moving our wine party to outdoors.

Su and Fifi sing duet:
Singing heartily, we shall get drunk among the blossoms!

Say, my husband, if it’s someone worth meeting, I

really! You would be unpardonably vile and cruel if

Liu:

What a strange dream I just had, in which my darling

gouged. Oh, I couldn’t bear seeing you suffering so!

Liu:

I also dreamed just now that you’d taken me to court

on me.

punishment in my place.

Anytime you are to suffer any pain, my dear, I'll

Liu:

So you ARE a sweet man, after all.

Jichang:

As your body takes the beating, my heart actually
feels the pain, my dear.

Liu:

But you’ll soon forget what you’ve just said.

昭君出 塞
Lady Glory’s Trek to the North

Lady Glory’s Trek to the North
(Enter two civil and two military officers.)
(Recitative:) With the patriotic phoenix
Has spring arrived in full glory;
Since the beginning of man’s history;
Has fealty to King been life’s radix.

Officer A:

Greetings to you all, Sirs.

Other officers: Greetings, Sir.
Officer A:

You have all received the order from His Majesty
to see Lady Glory off to the northern Huns at the
Pavilion of Leave-taking, I trust. Let us get on the
road, shall we, Sirs?

Others:

Indeed Sir, let’s.

Civilian officers: Imperative it is that using our brushes we acquire
accord.
Military officers:Our sacred duty is to bring peace with the sword!
(Exeunt all.)
(Enter Wang Long, brother of Lady Glory.)
(Recitative:)
Wang:

As so weak is the army,
Shriveled our territory.
Nothing has been done
About the barbarous Huns.

With no defense plans drawn,
The king does none but frown.

O Damn the black-hearted Mao Yanshou - he’s so evil;

Sending my lady to the Huns is too uncivil!
And damn the ministers, everyone a spineless buffoon!

Iam Wang Long, younger brother of Lady Glory.

Separation.

Court ministers ever sit waiting for time to slip away.

Servants:

Time slipping away.

Wang:

So-called warriors drop dead, one after another, every
day!

Yes, every day!

Drop dead and turn stiff, I pray, every one of them rats!

Servants:

Rats, O every one of them rats!

Drop dead, I say, rats, rats, rats!

Thus making me a pawn in this game merciless!

Though she’s traveling to the north,
Her heart is not moving forth.

Ending up thus in Hun territory.

desultory!

The Sire had to send her off on this dark, mission.

See but wild geese above fleeing the North
In icy gust that cuts like a sword!
The wilderness is covered with dark. clouds,

Ass rain falls, touching a sad chord!
How I remember well those halcyon days in the palace Days all gone now, and in misery I find no solace!
O, what misfortune has brought me to this!
Id never dreamed of all this wretchedness!
(Recitative:)
Glory:

Brother dear, can YOU still see our homeland?

Wang:

Yes, Your Ladyship, sister dear, but only if I strain my

eyes.
Glory:

Turn my horse around, livery boy.

Glory sings: Farewell, my beloved mother and father, farewell!
When will I ever see you again: I cannot tell!
O how I now yearn to see you, dears, one more time,

But here only mist and dust your child’s face begrime!
Al that’s in sight:
River Han floning endlessly on,
Asters growing wild hither and yon!
Homeland, in vain I try to find you on the horizon,
Alas, I see all around me but many a mountain!
Carrying only pipa, I bid you adieu, Your Majesty,
As I now brave the grim west wind in a land so dusty.
How could you, Emperor of Han, watch me
Face my undeserved, cruel destiny!
O how in silence I grieve,
As the fog brings no reprieve!
Curse you, Mao Yanshou, I do: you baleful man!
Its you who made me leave the land of the Han!
Ai future return to my homeland
Can only take place in a dreamland!

昭君出 塞
Lady Glory’s Trek to the North

Two thoughts fill my mind:
Your Majesty, begrudging you I do!
My beloved parents, how I miss you!
I had expected to be with my family my whole life long,
Little had I known that one day we'd sing this sad
parting song!
(Shouts of the Hun soldiers are heard.)
Glory sings: Lord, how terrifying is the sound of the Hun drums;
And how frightful to look at are the Hun bums!
Are those Hun horses or but evil creatures?
Are those Hun banners, or some hellish features?
Close up, more unsightly are the bee-like Hun soldiers,
Everyone revolting to the eye -- what vile pictures!
O how those proboscises are like vultures’ beaks,
And how those unkempt beards resemble wild, chopped
leeks!
Tell them what I say,
That they must make way!
(Recitative:)
Glory:

O how I harbor five profound regrets in my heart!

Wang:

Do tell me them, my dear sister, Your Ladyship.

Glory sings: Furstly, I can never for our parents’ love repay;
And then I resent in the Hun’s land 11 ever stay!
Thirdly, the defense of our borderlands is so weak,
Fourthly, the picture of our dynastic strength looks
bleak;
And lastly the thousands of our fighting men
Are nothing but groups of local patrol men!
Ifear Han Empire’ sacrifice of me, Glory,
Wil remain a dark spot in the Han history!
(Recitative:)
Wang:

Dear sister, Your Ladyship, what good would further
lamentation do? It’s more important that you will take
care of your health hereafter.

(Enter Sekong,)

Sings:

Once there was a monk named Sighting Lotus;
To save his mother he travelled to the Gate of Hell.
How far is it to Mount Spiritus?
Over one hundred eight thousand miles to you I tell.

(Recitative:) Woe is me! - with my head shaven bare
Next to me is nothing but a lone lamp
Time flies on, and so - Alas! - does my
With it my beauty too will vanish from

sight.

Yours truly is but a pitiful little nun.
Zhao is my family name, and my Buddhist given
name is Sekong (“Vanity is libido”). Sent while still
in childhood to this Xian Tao (“Celestial peach” in
translation, but the two characters also sound like

“first escape.”) Nunnery, I’ve been a nun. It’s bad
enough I do nothing but burn incense and chant
scriptures day in and day out. At night, however, I
suffer an even worse misery by sleeping alone in that
bloody cold bed. How, pray tell, am I to endure this

much longer?

Sings:

At the height of my girlish beauty | am sixteen years of age.
Pity me for allmy hair was shaven off by my pulpiteer mistress.
Burning incense and pouring sacred water at the altar
are my chores from one_year to the other.
Outside the nunnery gate the other morning I caught
sight of some young monks.
What do you know, one gave me the once-over when I
cast him no more than a casual glance!
Mercy! I reckon this must be what they call “love at first sight.”
For now I know if he and I can make a pair,
I wont care if we get sent to the King of Hell after death.
And for punishment, let us in the netherworld be:
Pounded by hammers,
Severed by saws;

Crushed by grinding stones,
Fried in sizzling oil!
When you think of it, have any of these punishments
been witnessed?
All we see is the living, suffering terribly.
So why worry about it and not let tomorrow take its own

Whatever we can enjoy right now, we should - and I’m

(Recitative:) You see, my gentle folks, it wasn’t my own choice to
be a nun.

Sings:

It all started with my parents:
My old man was always keen on Buddhist scriptures,
And my Mummy so very fond of chanting them.
Praying and chanting was all they ever did, night and day;
“Merciful Buddha!” was all they ever muttered day and night!
Just my luck, that I should be born a sickly child,
For that, fancy, they sent me to this nunnery
To be a nun under Buddha’ protection, so they say!
Now hear this:
I attend funerals as my daily routine,
For all the wretched lifeless stiffs.
Chanting endlessly, ringing bells ceaselessly,
While drums and gongs are played My life is being wasted away on these tedious ceremonies,
Just to escort those poor souls to the Lower World, so they say!
And the scriptures - you name it, I've learned to chant it The hardest must be the seven scrolls of the bloody Lotus
Scripture,
Which my mistress would force upon me
Even as I tried to get a little shut-eye;
Or as I dozed off into some naughty dream, if youll
pardon me!
Enough of this. I reckon I had better chant a bit now:
Amitabha, Merciful Buddhal...Amitabhal...
Hang you all, you matchmaking crones who have not
served Me...
Mercy! What am I chanting? ... Merciful Buddha,
Amitabhal...
O, but how can I chant when I harbor such agony!

(Recitative:) Thus pondering my life, my sorry state of mind has
grown even more vexed. It wouldn’t do me any harm
to go to the corridors for a stroll, I imagine.

Sings:

May a stroll in the corridors relieve some of my sorrow.

(Recitative:) Just you see: the Arhat Gods on both sides of the
corridor. Lord, don’t they look simply splendid!
_ Sings:

How interestingly sculptured are these Arhat Gods!
This one with one hand around his knee and the other
patting his tummy,
Is he calling out to me?
This one with hands holding his chins.
Is he worrying about me?
This one with his eyes sleepily half-closed
Is he peeping at me?
Now this one, I’m certain, with his silly mouth wide open,

Is grinning at me.
Surely he’s sneering that I’m letting time slip me by;
For once the sweet bird of youth has flown away,
Who, but who, pray, will want me, by then an old hag?

Just look, this one is angry at me,
And this one is reprimanding me,
And this one is weeping for me Weeping for my bleak and barren destiny.
This much I do know:
The altar lamps are never the same as the red candles in
a bridal suite;

Nor is a vegetarian meal the same as a cozy dinner

shared by a happy couple;
And the nunnery bell tower is surely not the same as a
warm chamber where a woman awaits her man;

Even less is a straw mat in a nunnery the same as the
soft bedding for a man and his nife.
Now will you look at me, gentle folks - am I a sappy
lass, or not?

Surely all can see I'm no ladde,
So why do I have on this yellow sash and loose robe made
for a man?

Fleeing down the Mountain
(Enter Benwu.)
Benwu sings: O/, I’m a_ pitiful, little acolyte monk!

But why on earth am I an acolyte monk?
How bitterly have I suffered all these years
From the abuses inflicted by my pulpiteers.
Fleeing the monastery is what I’m planning;
To my native land will I be returning.
Just think, my gentle folk:
In one or two years IU grow me a fine crop of hair;
In three or four, IV build a cabin, simple but fair;
In five or six, Ill find me a lass with whom to pair;
In seven or eight, a big, fat baby shed better bear;

And in nine or ten will my child call me Daddy;
“Daddy,” it will say, “oh, my Monk-ie Daddy!”
Joy will then be me,
Joy will then be me!
(Recitative:) Doing laundry is too much a bore,
Washing up is no less a chore.
All Buddhas exist but in a no man’s nether-land,

For scriptures a pious fool would brave quicksand.
A pitiful acolyte monk I am. Benwu (Nothingness in
English), my gentle folk, is my name. My ill fortune
started the day I was sent to this bloody temple to be a
monk. For from that moment on I have had to practice
the five frigging abstentions: no wine, no meat, et
cetera, et cetera. My job it is to tend to keep the incense
burning, do the mopping and sweeping, and chant the
scriptures every day - none of these bloody chores fills
my heart with the slightest gaiety. The master pulpiteer
and all the other little monks are on an outing today.
Even the second chef is off to fetch some firewood.
Why don’t I take a stroll outside the temple? I reckon I'll
do just that... Hey, just look at all this:
“The orioles are flying in pairs, and the swallows as
couples are building their nests; the butterflies are

Into the storage room I lock, up the tools, en toto.
No more Buddhist enlightenment, ifyou please;
For what good will ever come from attaining Holiness?
Having no son and having no wife
Aint what Id call a rewarding life!
How, pray tell, does a temple differ from a slammer?
So dont blame me for breaking out with a hammer.
Do _you not see how quickly the years go by?
The sweet bird of youth so quickly will fly.
I shall, however, remember the Shepherd Boy and the
Weaving Maid,
My future woman and I will emulate the hard work they
portrayed.
Thus, a happy life can be obtained, I trust;
Yes, a happy life can be obtained, it must!

The Mountain Gate
(Enter Lu Zhishen.)

Lusings:

See: what rich foliage,
What painting-like peaks!
A\ beautiful scenic spot this is, indeed!

(Recitative:)
Lu:

But why, O why, is there no wine?

Lusings:

How this wine-less life ires me!
And tt only gets worse everyday.

(Recitative:)
Lu:

I’ve put on a monk’s apparel;
But my heart plays its same old role.
No prayers and repentence,
I’m ever a fun-loving soul!
Yours truly goes by the name of Lu Zhishen.It’s been
nearly one whole year since I had my head shaven
and came to this temple.
O how I miss the days when I downed as much wine
and meat as my heart desired!
But what kind of life am I leading now? Dll tell you:
I’m bound by those five frigging rules, rules of
abstinence they call them.
Just look at me, will your Haggard and low-spirited
now, all because of the bloody vegetables fed to
me day after day.
How long can I go on like this? Not much longer, let
me tell you!
What good would it do me, even if I reached that
bloody - what do they call it - “nervouna?”
I’m going to take a stroll down the mountain out of
this disgusting hole, just to lift my spirit a bit.
Look, the distant peaks are adorned by floating
clouds, as the sun shines radiantly on the mountain
creeks. What a lovely sight!

(Recitative by Wine Peddler backstage.)
Peddler:
Wine for sale, everyone!

I AM tired, climbing all the way up here. I reckon I
shall rest a bit.

Lu:

Say, my good man, are those two barrels of decent wine?

Peddler:

They sure are!

Lu:

Where are you headed with it?

Peddler:

I’m going up the mountain to sell it.

Lu:

So you are selling it to the monks?

Peddler:

I’m selling it to those labourers working in the
mountain.

Lu:

What’s wrong with selling it to the monks?

Peddler:

No way!

Lu:

Why not?

Peddler:

Let me tell you, Master. The old abbot of your temple
loaned me the money for my business; he is also my
landlord. If he ever catches me selling my wine to any
of you, he is sure to ask for his money back, and he
is sure to kick me out of his house. Monks who buy
my wine are sure to be punished, too.

Why don’t you rest a while?

Good heavens, you are a good master! Say, may I
know your name?

The Peony Pavilion

A Stroll in the Garden
(Enter Du Liniang.)
Du sings:

My dreams softly fading, | awoke to...W hat music?
Tis thee, Oriole, thy singing so sweet and melodic!
Again, spring has stirred all nature -- how time does fly!
Yet, still deep within the walls of this mansion, stand I.

(Enter Fragrance, Du’s maid.)
Fragrance sings:
Having emptied the incense burner of its ashes,

I discarded, too, remnants of embroidery thread.
Spring has come, but no news can be happy, alas,
to my mistress who laments the time that so quickly has sped!
(Recitative:)
Fragrance:

Greetings, Miss.

(Recitative:)
Du:

Morning again, is it not, bringing with it but more

longing? In languish, I leave my coiffure unattended.
Fragrance:

Here you are alone, Miss, leaning by the window, with

your hair languorously wafted.
Du:

Oh, these nameless sorrows! It’s no use trying to rid
myself of them, and how they only deepen and will
not be a thing of past!

Fragrance:

But, Miss, I have told all the song birds, as you
instructed, that they should cherish the spring days
while they last.

(Recitative:)
Du:

Have you spoken to the gardener about sweeping the
petals off the paths, Fragrance?

Fragrance:

Yes, Miss, I have.

Du:

Fine.Bring me my robes and the mirror stand, then.

The Peony Pavilion

Fragrance:

Yes, Miss.

As her cloud-like coiffure is reflected in the mirror,

Scents are doubled, for my Lady does her robes savor.
Here they are, Miss.
Du:

Set them down, will you?

Yes, Miss.

My, what a lovely day it is...!

Du sings:

See wisps of luminescent smoke drifting lazily into my
lonesome court;

Spring O Spring, how carelessly have you meandered in
to cavort!
Luistlessly do I tend to my coiffure...
Why, in thee, Mirror, I can see only part of my face
And my rich, ebony hair I find alluring but demure.
How Ive longed to take a stroll outside,
But feared itd bring social disgrace!
Fragrance sings:

Oh, Miss, how splendid you look in that fair apparel,
With your dazzling gems on, you are yourself a rare jewel!
Du sings:

Do you not know, my girl, that my nature is love of
beauty?

(Recitative by Fragrance: I beg my young lady to step out of her
boudoir.)

Du and Fragrance sing duet:
But, alas! Beauty ts all too brief, just as the spring that
will soon flee:
Yes, such beauty as that would startle a swan gracefully
sailing,
Or beauty as that would be envied by flowers fully
blossoming!
(Recitative:)
Fragrance:

Here we are at the gate of the garden. Won’t you step
inside, Miss?

Du:

Yes. Why, nearly in ruins are the painted and gilt
loggias;

OA, it is in vain this fleeting moment of beauty has come:
For the enjoyment of spring belongs in other halls.
OA, clouds, floating hither and yon, day and night:
How the emerald-green halls are silhouetted against thy
rosy light!
And how the fine mist, borne on a teasing breeze,
shrouds
the painted boats in distance, as if themselves on clouds!
Alas! Spring, none of your riotous beauty
Is for those in locked mansions to see!

Yes, the hills have turned green, but how they are covered

I see the rose mallows - blossoms heralding the end of

Pring, -

To whom the lingering and tender tufts of smoke wed!
Peonies, oh, thou Queen of many a blossom:
With springs passing, you too shall succumb.

The Peony Pavilion

An Interrupted Dream
(Recitative.)
Du:

Dusings:

Back I am from my surprise visit with you, Spring Yes, we met; but why did it happen all so soon? How
tenderly were we over each other lingering! What will
I do, when you sing your farewell tune? My, what a
languorous, lazy spring day]...
Why, this sudden torrent of emotions brought on by the
Season:
Are they passions, are they yearnings? - I know not the
reason.
Born I was into this eminent family, whose tradition
Is to marry me into a family of equally high position.

But waiting indefinitely for such a union ideal
Is quietly wasting away my golden years.
No one knows my dreams that seem to me all too real Hidden as they are, with my untold fears?
Only in dreams can I let my mind freely wander,
Yet dreams, with the passing of spring, only wither.
Yes, wither and wither, my dreams - to whom could I
tell?
How in secret I grieve; only Heaven knows my hell!
(Enter Liu Mengmei.)
(Recitative:)
Liu:

Young lady, my young lady! I have been looking
everywhere for you, and here you are! See,I have
plucked a branch off the weeping willow. As I know
you ate well-versed in poetry, why don’t you, my lady,
compose a poem about it?

Du: (To herself): A stranger this young man! How, I wonder, did
he come to be here?

Liu:

O my dear young lady, don’t you know that
deeply in love with your

牡丹 亭
The Peony Pavilion

Liu sings:

Red Are the Wild Peach Blossoms
Because for you, my flowerlike fair,
Years passing like the waters flow...
Sought you everywhere I have,
And at last I find you here,
Alone in a room full of woe.

(Recitative:)
Liu:

My dear lady, let’s have a tete-a-tete over there, shall we?

Du:

But...why...and where...where shall we go?

Liu sings:

O my dear...
Let us stroll, round the fence for the peonies, to behind
the rockery,
Where I shall your collar unbutton, and your robes
unite...
Please, bite into your sleeves,

my little virginal Dove...
Endure just a moment, ere you enjoy

the sweetness of love!
(Du and Liu sing Duet, )
Yes, we met before, somewhere, sometime,

Seeing you again is an encounter sublime.
How could we have known:
words would be unneeded
when to each other we greeted?
(Exeunt.)

(Reenter Liu and Du.)

Liu sings:

O my! Even Nature was on our side:
In the flowering shrubs, our love-shrine, did we hide.
My beloved: your cloudlike hair falling in disarray,
Your gorgeous jewelry holds sway.
How I would ever cling to you,
Never would we bid one another adieu!
How I would that we were always one in flesh,
And ever would we in spirit too enmesh!
O, my bold words have reddened your cheeks in strain,
But you look more like a lovely flower in a fine rain!

who will come to my rescue?...
Rest or rise? - I wish I Rnem...
More sleep may be what I best ensue.
Fatigued and languid from strolling in the garden,
No scented satin covers do I need at present for a repose.
Be not yet too far away, I hope, my dream forbidden!
And Spring: take me back into the dream sweet like a
rose!

The Peony Pavilion

Dreamland Revisited
(Enter Du Liniang.)

Du sings:

Oh, Spring, how especially alluring you are this year!
Why, despite these walls and fences, you have entered even
my quarters!
Awakening the irrepressible human yearnings of
Springtime;

See how the idly blossoming rose mallows grasp at my
Skirts.
Indeed, your force caresses both blossoms and human
hearts.

(Recitative:)

Strolling down the paths amidst all these spring
sights, I can’t help remembering my dream last night.
How quiet it is herel... Just the right time to revisit my
dreamland...

Sings:

Yes, there are the rockeries beyond;
And here I am at the Peony Pavilion.
By the carved railings the peonies are still in flower;
Like silky threads, the leaves of weeping willows wave;
Resembling coins of gold, the elm seeds twinkle.
But surely no gold is as precious as this glory of spring.

(Recitative:) How memorable, what that young scholar and I did
in my dream last night...!

Sings:

Furst he was still leaning against the rockery,
And I, a shy, young woman next to him on my feet.
Gently, he then embraced me,

Such was his ardor for union.
Holding each other, we withdrew behind the railings,
Around the swings.
Hesitantly, I disrobed;

On the lawn we lay How Ifeared that heaven would see us!
AM this ts still so clear to me How indescribable was the subtle wonderment!

(Recitative:) But how did all the petals come to shower on us at

Excited and euphoric he was,
Biting tenderly on my shoulders;

Passion so overwhelming,
Passion that rendered me limp and faint!
But, why were all those petals falling from the sky,
Or was I confused, because it was but a dream?
(Recitative:) But where has all this gone? The pavilion by which
the peonies blossomed and the railings on which wild

AMI see now is a waste land,

With the pavilion and loggias deserted.
Can it be I’m too blinded by love to find
What was yesterday so distinct,
And my dream will never come again?
But clearly it all did happen.
Or maybe I should linger a bit longer to find all that...
For I am certain this is the spot we shared our love.
(Recitative:) Oh, my young scholar, where can you be?

grow numerous plums. A pretty tree it is! How lovely
it would be if I could be buried beneath it when I
depart from this world!
Sings:

Suddenly my heart is laden with sadness,
Standing thus by the plum tree.
Oh, there would be none of this grieving and lamenting,
If only we could be free to love anything as we do nature,
And free to choose how and when to live and die!
Would that, here, after death my spirit could remain,

Finding a Portrait
(Enter Liu Mengmei.)
Sings:

Spring O Spring, why have you crept up on me unguarded,
Me, a wayfarer who such matters had long disregarded?

(Recitative:) Quietly in flower, the pear trees perfume the yard.
To whom could I tell the passage of yet another
lonely year?
See all the countless willow leaves, like my thoughts O, so unclear!

Dancing as sirens, how they tease the lovelorn bard!

(Recitative:) | am yours truly, Liu Mengmei. Struck by
ailment I took refuge in the Apricot Blossom
Temple. Luckily, I was treated by Mr. Chen, a
of mine and a fine, recommended physician.
a few days of medication and rest, I trust
recovered. In spirit, however,

some
Taoist
friend
After
I am

I am pervaded by an

unknown melancholy, perhaps brought on by the
advent of spring. What indeed is to be done? This
morning, Auntie Stone has mentioned to me a garden
nearby, which is not unlike a park with much to be
seen and enjoyed. It might do me good to go and
take a stroll in it. Walking in slow paces because of
my weakened condition, here I am finally at the west
corner of the garden. Hm... a bamboo fence with such
rich growth of plants on it... But O what a pity, so
dilapidated it is! Let me step inside and have a look...
Gracious!
The worn railings remain those of jadite;
The fallen walls are now too sad a sight.
Their times of splendor proved all too brief to last;
Like smoke, like mist, so the golden days are past.
Oh, what a garden!
Sings:

Clearly, whether glory or romance, with time they wear
away.

The Peony Pavilion

Along what must once have been fine walls I'm finding my
way.
The path, covered with moss, makes easy a slip - wellnigh!
Collapsed the once resplendent tower in ruins lie;
Since when have the lacquered doors fallen in fungi?
(Recitative:)I see that this garden was once frequented by
countless guests, which explains all these names

engraved on the bamboo trees.
Sings:

People came - now gone; once they cared.
Year in, year out, so time too fared.
Leave on the trees they did
Their names, now all faded.

(Recitative:) O My! Here is a pile of rare stones... from the

ravaged rockery, no doubt. What a pity it is!..Wait,
what is this under the stones? Let me pick it up and
see. Why, it is a scroll. Pll unroll it and inspect. My,
it must be a portrait of Guan Yin, the Goddess of
Mercy. A blissful finding for me, this must be. But
how on earth did it get to be cast here under the
stones? Hm....I think I ought to bring it back to
my studio, where I should pay Her due respect by
burning some incense and say a few prayers to Her
everyday. Can’t leave it here under the stones, can I?
I do trust ’m doing the right thing.
Sings:

Buried under the ruined rockery
Is Guan Yin, the beautiful fairy,
Inside the garden alone,
Under the ravaged stone.
Why, this must be a god-send;
To bless me she’s come to tend.
So would you stay o'er my incense burner?
And allow me to be your pious server?
Burning incense and lighting the lamp - all for you, my
lady.
Would you in return kindly bestow upon me some mercy?
Ai fair deal I trust it to be,

So pray, my lady, answer me!
(Exit Liu Mengmei.)

Zhong Kui’s Earthly Mission

Zhong Kui’s Earthly Mission
Act One
(Enter Head of Ghosts.)
Head sings: May health, wealth and mirth
Come to all on earth,

At the new year’s birth.
Hark the exciting sounds of firecrackers
Set off by celestial merrymakers!
(Recitative:)
Head:

I am Head of Ghosts

serving Master Zhong Kui,

Grand Marshal of the netherworld. I don’t know
why, but Master Zhong ordered me to have all the
musicians ready, and prepare other cultural relics. His
order is of course my command. Well, here comes
my Master.
(Zhong Kui enters.)

Zhong:

“With gumption I traveled to the capital,
Where I aspired to pass the examination.
Little had I expected it to be a trip fatal,
For there I met my greatest humiliation.”
I am Zhong Kui of the Eternal South Mountain. I
passed the imperial examination in the capital. Who
would have thought that the hateful and narrowminded

emperor disqualified me, all for what he

called my iess than presentable countenance. In great
agony, I killed myself. Thanks to my loyal and dear
friend Master Du Ping that I was properly buried
and became Grand Marshal of the netherworld.
While alive I promised my little sister’s hand to Du
Ping, who is learned, young and handsome. Though
dead now, I shall honor my promise. I have ordered
my men to get the musicians ready, and prepare
a precious sword and a case of fine books as her
dowry. This evening, accompanied by these little
ghosts and carrying all her dowries, I'll take my little

the air.
All the plum blossoms add to the beauty of the season, I

Not as white as the snow, the blossoms emit rich fragance
50 Yare.
Both true representatives of purity, the snow and the
blossom,

Not only are they the essence of winter for many songsters
to hum,

But also symbols of purity poets over the centuries declare
to be.
(Recitative:)

Ghost A:

We are approaching your home village, Master.

Zhong:

You all wait for me outside the village gate.

Ghosts:

Yes, Master.

(Exeunt Ghosts.)

Zhong sings: See the oon seems sad hiding behind the clouds,
Yes, she’s hiding behind the clouds.
Back from the dark and desolate netherworld,
I’m at my earthly home: now a sight of ruin unfurled!
(Recitative:)
Zhong:

Dear little sister, open the door, please.

Ms. Zhong: Just a moment, please.

Alt his house will this tdeal wedding be held.

Ass I've known him to be since childhood.

Act Four
(Enter Fragrance.)
(Recitative:)
Fragrance:

Do please come out, Master Du.

(Enter Du.)

Du:

Fragrance, what are you doing back here?

Fragrance:

At the command

of Master

Zhong

Kui, I’ve

accompanied his sister, Mistress Zhong, here to be
married to you, Master.
Du:

What! Master Zhong Kui is here? Where, but where?

(Enter Zhong Kui and entourage.)
Zhong:

Greetings, my dear friend Du Ping.

Du:

But who’s speaking, and where are you ?

Zhong:

Don’t be alarmed, my friend. 1 AM Zhong Kui. I
have not forgotten all the kindnesses you showed me
while I was alive. I also remember that I promised
you my little sister’s hand in marriage. This is why
I’ve come back, just to see the two of you happily
married. Here I am with my sister and all these
cultural relics as her dowry.

Du:

But why can’t I see you, my friend?

Zhong:

You are still in the human world, but I’m only a spirit

now. We cannot see each other. Please get ready for
the wedding.
Du:

But I need some time for that.

(Enter Butler.)

Butler:

Master, I’ve everything well prepared.

Du:

Bring me the wedding robe, then. And show Ms.
Zhong, my fiancee, in.

Zhong Kui’s Earthly Mission

(Enter Ms. Zhong.)
Butler:

Now the wedding ceremony begins. Follow my
instructions, please, the newly-weds:
First, bow to all gods who preside over this ceremony;
Bow then to your parents who upon this union agree.
Now bow to each other as loving man and wife;
Before entering the bridal suite to begin a sweet life!

(Exeunt Du and Ms. Zhong and Fragrance.)

Zhong:

Bravo, bravo! Now that my little sister is all settled
down with my fine friend Du Ping, I have no more
earthly worries. Say, my little ghosts, let’s go!

Zhong sings: See all the lights glitter;
Yes, how they glitter!
See all the candles in shades pure silver,
And all the banners gloriously shimmer:
Symbols of serenity;
Symbols of felicity!
Music and drum are playing a symphony;
Yes, how they are played in harmony!
All 1s auspiciousness, all 1s ecstasy!
With my sister married, happiness ts me!
(Exeunt Zhong Kui and entourage.)

The Palace of Eternal Youth

Chamber of Frivolous Commotions
(Enter Gao Lishi.)
(Recitative:)
Gao:

Year after year since being made imperial consorts,
Many of them weep until they run out of tears!
As beautiful as they may all be,
Some are happy and others, in misery.
I am Chief Eunuch, Gao Lishi. Years ago I was in the

Fujian province, where I chose a beauty named Jiang
to be a consort to His Majesty. She became his pet
lady, and was bequeathed by His Majesty the title of
Lady Mei, which means plum blossom, her favorite
flower. Thereafter we all addressed her as Lady Mei,
instead of Lady Jiang. Later, however, Lady Yang
came to serve our Lord, and she took Lady Mei’s
place as His Majesty’s favorite consort. Before long,
His Majesty moved Lady Mei out of his inner palace
and set her up inside the eastern chamber, where

she was all but forgotten. Suddenly, last night, His
Majesty claimed sick to the others and spent the night
at the western chamber, where he asked me to bring

to him Lady Mei. His Majesty specifically told me to
keep it as a secret to Lady Yang, This is why I’m here
guarding this quarters, so that no one other than the
staff can pass through. Look, it’s almost daybreak. It
may be time to take Lady Mei back to her quarters. I’ll
wait here for any orders from His Majesty.
(Exit Gao. Enter Lady Yang.)

Yang sings: After a restless night with a troubled mind,
Ive sneaked in here before it’s light.
Normally, when the blossoms were playing with their
shadow in the sunlight,
I would stall be half asleep, dozing off some more under
my silk, blanket.
But today, I couldnt wait to push away my embroidered
pillow,

The Palace of Eternal Youth

Yang:

A totally unworthy creature that I am, I have been
undeservedly in Your Majesty’s favor for a while
now. It has dawned on me that if I do not withdraw
myself from Your Majesty right now, Your Majesty
will suffer from slanderous rumors and criticisms
from the whole country, saying that I, your unworthy
slave, am the cause of your betraying those whom
you love. That would truly make ever more severe my
crime of being such an undeserving wretch. I hereby
beseech Your Majesty to take pity on my situation
and dismiss my poor and unneeded service and send
me out of your sight, and out of your mind forever.
And then Your Majesty can shower all your true love
on those who are far more deserving than I am!

Yang sings: dow me to pay the highest, yes, the highest, respect to

Grateful as I am to your kindness, my Lord,
I know too well that I do NOT deserve these gifts!
(Recitative by Emperor: How can you say these things, my love!)

You and I are a truly loving couple,
Truly we are!
I wish to spend the rest of my life, and beyond, with you.
So how can you speak of separation,
How can you!
I am to blame for all this,
I alone am to blame!
Please be hurt no more,

No more!
Why, I never loved you more than now
When you're looking so delicately vulnerable!

Startled by the Rebellions
(Enter Gao Lishi.)
(Recitative:)
Gao:

Hear how music and songs from the heavenly towers
softly arise;
Hark how gentle breezes with laughter of court ladies
fuse crosswise!
Shadows dance within the moonlit palace as night
wears away,
Crystal-like bamboo blinds seem so, so near the
autumn Milky Way.
I am Gao Li-shi,

a humble servant to His Majesty,

the Emperor. At the order of His Majesty, I have
arranged this intimate dinner party for the enjoyment
of His Majesty and Her Royal Highness, Lady Yang,
To serve and to please are my sole duties tonight.

(Enter Emperor Ming Huang and Lady Yang, and entourage. )

Duet:

Faintly azure is the sky,
Clouds float languidly high.
Sailing southward in queues through the infinite,
Rove none but wild geese making their aerial extt.
What a delight is this earthly garden:
all these glorious hues of autumn.
The weeping willows turned golden,
Light green become the sweet alyssum.
Fuchsia petals of water lily,
Drifting apart in melancholy.
Fresh cassias by the gilded balustrade,
In cool air subtle fragrance they pervade.

(Recitative:)
Yang:
Your Majesty!

Emperor:

“My beloved lady, I do wish to take a stroll with you.

After you, Your Majesty.

Your statuesque being,
my love, fair and lustrous!
(Recitative:)
Emperor:

Have some mote wine, my beloved lady.

Yang:

You, too, Your Majesty.

Yang:

Bottoms up.

Emperor:

Yes, bottoms up. At such a quiet and intimate dinner
party, my lady, I have no wish for music and songs
that we listen to everyday. All I yearn to hear is the
song based on the poem, “Tunes of Lucidity and
Serenity,” by Li Bai, who composed it some years
ago during a spring party at the Pavilion of Depth of
Fragrance, celebrating the blossoming peonies - the
poem for which my court musician Li Guinian later
wrote music at my order. You do remember the poem
and the music, my beloved, do you not?

Yang:

I remember it well.

Emperor:

Do sing it for me then, won’t you?

Yang:

Your wish is my command, Your Majesty.

Emperor:

I shall beat the rhythm to your singing, my love.

Yang sings:

All you lovely blossoms in rich profusion how you remind one of her radiance;
Why, you light clouds of heavenly vision copy you do her robes of resplendence!
Who could ever be comparable to beauty of her sort?
None but the languid Lady Lark of the Han court.
She, a lady of looks of a sweet and precious flower,
as sure to earn a pleased gaze from her master-emperor.
So while bidding sad farewells in this pavilion to the
Spring so soon passing,
Rejoice too should we at our being one, leaning thus
against the fine railing.

(Recitative:)
Emperor:

Brava! Brava! What pleasure to have this combination,
The unsurpassed literary genius of Li Bai blended
with the peerless voice of my beloved.

I present the wine to you, my love.
But I'm afraid my tears will fill the cup,
In which therell be no room for the fine spirit!
Come, my darling, and enjoy this first cup of wine.
Ah, but you're dead - you died in that run-down
roadhouse!
You died in the riotous chaos and was buried without a
Juneral!
I didn?t even present to_your ghost a cup of water, let
alone wine!
Oh, how I wouldve liked to put to death all of those
rebels,

And only then should you, one who died for her country,
be buried.
Facing you like this,
I know that though looking alive,
You are sad and long dead!

(Recitative by Gao: Present the second cup of wine, Your Majesty.)
I present to_you, my love, this cup of emerald-green wine,
On this long, dark night as endless as my sorrows.
My world has been lightless since you died, leaving me

Your statue, my darling, will remain here, for now, in the
southwest,

Until someday, I Rnow not when, youll go back with me
to the north.
There one day we will be properly buried together as
husband and wife,
In death we will turn into two trees with branches
intertwined,

And into a pair of lovebirds that will nest at our grave.
(Gao: My Lord, present the third cup of wine.)
With this cup of wine I've ended the funeral ritual to
you, my pet.

But the words I wish to whisper into_your sweet ears will
never end.
Why, my love, are there no expressions in your eyes?
Cant you see how haggard I’ve turned since you were
gone?
Oh, it was because I was cowardly unprotective of you,
That you died by hanging - oh, how you were made to
die!
AMI can do now is to blame and torment myself,
Uniil I feel as though my head were slashed open,
And my insides set on violent fire!

Yes, that is the same sad look as the one on her before

Poor, old Gao Lishi is on his knees now,

And your old maid-servants prostrate themselves in
SOYVOW.
They are hoarse from crying out for you, my dear,
And all of them looking at each other helplessly in
despair.
They cry for you, for you were so, so kind to all of them,
Which makes their loving memory of you seem more
unendurable.
Outside the wind is blowing long, wailing sound,
The nightingale who lost its mate letting out sad shrieks,
Ass the dying sunset lingers on the lower side of the wall.
(Recitative by Gao: Please burn some paper money for Lady Yang
now, Your Majesty.)
See this mountain-like pile of paper money.
On fire, it’s turned into thousands of pieces of ashes.
But I know money can not bring back your spirit.
My robes are wet with tears shed for our regrets,
AMs I see all the ashes fly about like butterflies in pairs!
Oh, what agony!
Would that we could meet again
Ass those fortunate lovers in myths!

Gao:

It’s getting late, my Lord. Please return to the palace,

Your Majesty.
Emperor:

You may pull the ceremonial drape over Lady Yang’s

长 生殿
‘The Palace of Eternal Youth

A Tale Told on the Pipa
(Enter Li Guinian, the master musician.)
(Recitative:)
Li:

Since the rebel forces rose in Yuyang (Beijing),
The palaces in Chang’an have turned into a waste
land.
Only this old, survived musician from the past
has composed the end of an era into a sad song.
I am Li Guinian, once an imperial musician serving
His Majesty in the Imperial Academy of Music and
Drama. I was His Majesty’s favorite, bless His soul!
I still remember how I composed “The Resplendent
Robes” for His Majesty, which so pleased my Lord
and Lady Yang they bestowed upon me thousands of
yards of the finest silk. Who would’ve guessed that
before long An Lushan led the rebel forces and took
over Chang’an, the capital. With His Majesty fleeing
to the west, the whole nation was left in chaos. All

the musicians and actors in the Academy fled in all
directions for safety. That is how I ended up here in
the land south of the Yangtze River. As a refugee, I’ve
spent the last tally of silver in my wallet. Now I’ve no
choice but play and sing on my pipa for money. Today
is the day of a big fair at the Eagles Peak Temple,
where there should be many local people and tourists
enjoying themselves. Let me walk there and make
a few tallies of silver for food. Oh, how can I help

thinking of the years
singing to the emperor
at me now, down and
begger. What a sad and
Li sings:

when I was composing and
in the heavenly palace! Look
out singing and playing like a
dreadful situation I’m in!

Never had I thought that bad times would strike in my
old days
And lead me to this current miserable and impoverished
Stale.

Months of being on the flight have turned my face
haggard

长 生殿

And my hair white like frost.
Now I'm just a drifter in these unknown lands,
With the pipa as my only constant companion.
Hiding my embarassment, I walk past the broad
boulevards
And the narrow lanes.
I'm no warrior like Gao, who became a vagabond singing
bravura songs.
Rather, I resemble Wu, the martyr, who, as a beggar,

played the flute.
I recall vividly how I played fine music inside the palaces,
And how I sang pretty songs to all those in the court.
Oh, the kindness I received from His Majesty was earthShaking!
He wanted me to perform at the spa, where the spring
glittered against the melting snow,
Alnd told me to sing by His side in a floating boat, next
to which water lilies bloomed;

I was given the order to perform, while He enjoyed the
moonlight.
Accompaning Him on his flowering viewing trip, I would
play.
Just as I was showered with all these favors,
Unprecedented tragedy of the state struck.
In fear and in despair, His Majesty fled to the west,
Where the lovely Lady Yang met with her cruel destiny.
Oh, so many deaths I witnessed on my way fleeing the
north!
Here I’m at the end of my rope,
And I’m playing songs, composed for His Majesty, on
the streets,

Most of which unappreciated by the masses.
Oh, facing these southern gardens and scenic spots,
I can only sigh!
(Exit Li Guinian. Enter Li Mo, a young scholar.)
(Recitative:)
Mo:

As the blossoms catch the eyes of the viewers,
Their hearts feel wounded by the loss of half of the
nation.

The Palace of Eternal Youth

I wish to know the whereabouts of the creator of

“The Resplendent Robes,”
For no one appreciates his talent more than I do.
My name is Li Mo. I spent a few years in the western
capital of Chang’an, and when the An Lu Rebellion
broke out, I returned to the south. While I was
in the capital, I heard, across a wall, the divinely

beautiful music of “The Replendent Robes.” The
music was played by some member of the Imperial
Academy of Music. Though I tried to write down
the music as I was listening, I couldn’t get the whole

composition. Yesterday I was told that an elderly
musician was singing songs accompanied by his pipa
on the streets, and his artistry was as fine as the best
of the members of the Academy. He must be at the
grand fair at Hagle’s Peak Temple today, where the
fair always attracts many visitors. I just might find
him there. Yes, Pll go there...Now I’m on my way to
the fair. How exciting it is to see all the people and
boisterousness near the fair!

Strolling among the beauteous blossoms, we treasure

How we enjoy seeing all these visitors attracted by

We mustn’t waste our youth, dearie. Let’s have a good

must be here at the fair, we’ve come specifically to
hear him.

Visitor 1:

It was love at first sight on the part of our Sire, when he
Set eyes upon her.
Bestowing upon her precious gifts such as a jeweled box
and gold pins,
His Majesty named her the most gorgeous lady of them
all in his court.
(Recitative:)
Scholar:

Let’s have a description of the lady, shall we?

Visitor 1:

Was she as pretty as our girl here?

Visitor 2:

Sing to us what she looked like, will you?

Li sings:

Like a celestial beauty Lady Yang was.
Words cannot fully convey her loveliness.
Her face recalled flowers in bloom, her figure weeping
willow in the wind.
More beautiful than all belles of fame of antiquity,
Fler good looks surpassed those of ladies of myth.
Moving like a spring breeze,
Breathing like a spring blossom,
She existed like a spring dream!
Even the finest painter cannot capture her indescribable
charm!

(Recitative:)
Visitor 2:

My, my! The way you describe Lady Yang’s
attractiveness makes me feel as though she were right
in front of us. Do you all suppose this is nothing but
a fib?

Who cares, so long as it sounds like a good fib! Go
on, old singer. Tell us how the emperor treated her.

Li sings:

She was his rarest and most precious pearl,
Accompanying him all day, yes, all day long!
She was to him like the legendary Lady Zhao to the
Han emperor.
Like two lovebirds in the palace,
Inseparable from each other,
They almost breathed together all day and throughout the
night!

to the rebellion.

Mo:

But, sir, I think Lady Yang was not to blame. The
emperor appointed the wrong generals to the borders.
That’s what caused the country to see so much
chaos and war. If only he had employed some good
generals, situations would’ve been entirely different.

Scholar:

There’s much truth in your words.

Li:

Speaking of the rebellion that started in Beijing, it
was indeed a catastrophe that has caused so much
misery on the land. With your permission, I would
like to tell it to you through my music.

All:

Please do.

Just as the palace was galvanized by the song and dance
of “The Resplendent Robes,”
Who wouldve known that the sound of war drums
would start in Beijing?
When memorials reporting the rebellion were presented to
the emperor,
The whole palace was turned upside down, none knowing
what to do.
With His ministers screaming, frightened, flustered,
The emperor left the capital and headed west.
Fits royal palanquin was followed by that of his darling,
Lady Yang,

mutinous,

Where was Lady Yang buried?

By the rundown road-inn at the

She was a beauty nonpareil who
A\ tragedy made when her blood

(Recitative:)
Scholar:

What happened to Chang’an, after the rebel force had
entered it?

Li:

Oh, my dear people, the grand city of Chang’an was
all but ruined after that. Let me tell you more.

Li sings:

Since the emperor fled west to the Shu province,
Flow the soldiers caused destruction in Chang'an!
The government ceased to be,
Wtth its calm and prosperity!
Webcobs filled the palatial chambers,
Ass foxes wailed in the imperial quarters.
Oh, how wild partridges cried
In the rampant growth of reeds!
Mountain deer ran amok in the palace
Among blossoms that were half died!
Not one human in sight was there to clean up.

No, but he was my contemporary.

No. Though we both played pipa, the same instrument,

(Mo: Then I suppose you are Master Ma Xianqi.)

You're mistaken again. I’m not him.
But I knew all these men.
(Mo: How did you come to this southern city?)
As the country was half ruined and my family broken,
I fled the north and ended here in this unfamiliar
southern land.

Since you're so persistent,
IM tellyou Li Guinian is my name.
